<div id="review_252587364" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/252587364" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/140760-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1182311292p2/140760.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/252587364?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/140760-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer252587364" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10951675132955514511">I told the guy in the bookstore (whose name is also Daniel) that I wanted a book that would open my brain up. He didn't think too long before he pointed me towards this short weird book.<br/><br/>Imagine that David Lynch and Haruki Murakami got punchy one night and decided to write a noir detective novel together. And Samuel Beckett stopped by to contribute a chapter or two? I recognize this sounds crazy, but it's hard to imagine that this book was written by a single person. There are so many thoughts cr</span>
  <span id="freeText10951675132955514511" style="display:none">I told the guy in the bookstore (whose name is also Daniel) that I wanted a book that would open my brain up. He didn't think too long before he pointed me towards this short weird book.<br /><br />Imagine that David Lynch and Haruki Murakami got punchy one night and decided to write a noir detective novel together. And Samuel Beckett stopped by to contribute a chapter or two? I recognize this sounds crazy, but it's hard to imagine that this book was written by a single person. There are so many thoughts crammed into every page, and they're typically surprising and convoluted and odd.<br /><br />Let's start at the very beginning. Quinn writes pulpy detective novels under the pseudonym William Wilson. But really, he feels some significant connection to the protagonist of those novels, Max Work. And then one night, with nothing in particular going on, somebody calls him asking for Paul Auster, who is apparently a private detective. The caller desperately needs the help of Auster in order to prevent a murder. And Quinn at first is all like "I'm not Paul Auster", but then a little later he's all like "yeah, sure, I'm Paul Auster" and he jumps head first into doing some detective stuff.<br /><br />Then the book kind of gets odd.<br /><br />I feel pretty good about feeling confused, which means I tend to enjoy weird books and movies. And I will tell you right now that this book is confusing. Not in the same way that a Tolstoy novel is confusing, with everybody having like 3 names, or the way the first chapter of "Infinite Jest" is just totally opaque. The plot is crystal clear and it's very easy to tell what's happening, the above paragraph notwithstanding. "City of Glass" is confusing because Auster gives you so many things to think about on basically every page. He had a lot of ideas in his brain and wanted to share all of them with you. Sometimes this made the book feel disjointed, but most of the time it just felt rich.<br /><br />You should read this book if you like weirdo detective stories. And you should probably avoid this book if you really need to be able to decide on the significance of every literary curve ball Auster throws. It's a pretty short book, but it will support a lot of pondering. Just don't expect a lot of resolution.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10951675132955514511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_418906058" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/418906058" />
    <a title="Bandit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit"><img alt="Bandit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289488158p2/4528663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/418906058?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bandit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bandit" href="/user/show/4528663-bandit">Bandit</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17829019411146405874">I was torn between 3 and 5 stars and have settled on 4.<br/><br/>My 3 star review: This is solidly written. The prose is good but not breathtaking. The themes and premise are thought-provoking if slightly contrived, consciously self-conscious. I respect what Auster has done here. I can't say that the story resonated with me in a deeply personal way. There are parts I still don't quite understand.<br/><br/>My 5 star review: This library book had been calling me from the shelf for so long, I finally gave in and read </span>
  <span id="freeText17829019411146405874" style="display:none">I was torn between 3 and 5 stars and have settled on 4.<br /><br />My 3 star review: This is solidly written. The prose is good but not breathtaking. The themes and premise are thought-provoking if slightly contrived, consciously self-conscious. I respect what Auster has done here. I can't say that the story resonated with me in a deeply personal way. There are parts I still don't quite understand.<br /><br />My 5 star review: This library book had been calling me from the shelf for so long, I finally gave in and read the thing. As it turns out, this was the perfect time to read it, because this very eerie book very eerily corresponds to other things I've been reading lately. It's always a curious feeling when your books seem to be in dialogue with one another! Auster's novel ties itself in knots, making you think about language, identity, voice and authorship. There are parts I still don't quite understand.<br /><br />And finally...<br />My 4 star review: I don't think I'll read the rest of the trilogy, but I liked this and I'm glad I read it.</span>

<div id="review_2517116915" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2517116915" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33280608-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490807154p2/33280608.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2517116915?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/33280608-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33280608-ben?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33280608-ben?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33280608-ben?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2517116915" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6421490965021420327">City of Glass is the first installment of a 3 novel trilogy written by Paul Auster. Its basically a mystery à la hard-boiled noir fiction popular during the 50s. It tells the story of a detective fiction writer who embarks on a complicated case which involve a variety of odd characters. This adventure would release the protagonist of his daunting loneliness after the death of his wife and son, and would entail some passionate discussions about literature and theology. <br/><br/>This book was extremely enj</span>
  <span id="freeText6421490965021420327" style="display:none">City of Glass is the first installment of a 3 novel trilogy written by Paul Auster. Its basically a mystery à la hard-boiled noir fiction popular during the 50s. It tells the story of a detective fiction writer who embarks on a complicated case which involve a variety of odd characters. This adventure would release the protagonist of his daunting loneliness after the death of his wife and son, and would entail some passionate discussions about literature and theology. <br /><br />This book was extremely enjoyable. The prose is simply superb and seems to compliment the plot very well. I really enjoyed the description the author gave to either characters or places like this one for example: <br /> <i>"His hair was white, and it lay on his head uncombed, sticking up here and there in tufts. He was tall, thin, without question past sixty, somewhat stooped. Inappropriately for the season, he wore a long brown overcoat that had gone to seed, and he shuffled slightly as he walked. The expression on his face seemed placid, midway between a daze and thoughtfulness. He did not look at the things around him, nor did they seem to interest him. He had one piece of luggage, a once beautiful but now battered leather suitcase with a strap around it"</i><br /><br />Another admirable quality about this book is the personality of the protagonist. A methodical man who seems at once indifferent to life but one who is extremely concerned about his purpose in it. His internal monologues are enchanting. This one in particular when he pondered upon "fate": <br /> <br /> <i>"Was “fate” really the word he wanted to use? It seemed like such a ponderous and old-fashioned choice. And yet, as he probed more deeply into it, he discovered that was precisely what he meant to say. Or, if not precisely, it came closer than any other term he could think of. Fate in the sense of what was, of what happened to be. It was something like the word “it” in the phrase “it is raining” or “it is night.” What that “it” referred to Quinn had never known. A generalized condition of things as they were, perhaps; the state of is-ness that was the ground on which the happenings of the world took place." <br /></i><br /><br />In the end I didn't think I completely absorbed the full meaning of this book. There are many ideas which the author tried to convey but either failed to or it was for lack of contemplation on my part. Either way, it remains an interesting take on the classic noir mystery genre and a story which requires a second careful read, this time in hopes of absorbing its full content.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6421490965021420327" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1572553687" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1572553687" />
    <a title="Graham P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3924545-graham-p"><img alt="Graham P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515503736p2/3924545.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1572553687?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Graham P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Graham P" href="/user/show/3924545-graham-p">Graham P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1572553687" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5376485330356918010">A detective dime-store novel parading as an existential study about multiple identities. Paul Auster has written a strange, often times sad, short novel about a failed poet turned crime writer turned accidental private dick turned street-corner philosopher turned.... you get the idea. What's refreshing is that the novel is not concerned with solving a mystery, but actually making the mystery so thick and obtuse that the reader won't find an easy escape route. Auster throws in a whole array of re</span>
  <span id="freeText5376485330356918010" style="display:none">A detective dime-store novel parading as an existential study about multiple identities. Paul Auster has written a strange, often times sad, short novel about a failed poet turned crime writer turned accidental private dick turned street-corner philosopher turned.... you get the idea. What's refreshing is that the novel is not concerned with solving a mystery, but actually making the mystery so thick and obtuse that the reader won't find an easy escape route. Auster throws in a whole array of references; George Washington, Cervantes, the Tower of Babel, The Cherry Tree, The Garden of Eden; and while most of it could clutter any narrative with ungainly pretensions, it really works well in this framework. It's a subversively mundane detective novel full of eccentric turns and even more eccentric characters. Read this one alongside 'The Deadly Percheron' (Bardin) and 'Dreaming of Babylon' (Brautigan) for a class in deviations of detective pulp. (I think Luis Bunuel would have been a perfect director for a film version of this novel.)<br /><br />Plus, Auster knows how to write about New York City:<br /><br />"'I have come to New York because it is the most forlorn of places, the most abject. The brokenness is everywhere, the disarray is universal. You only have to open your eyes to see it. The broken people, the broken things, the broken thoughts. The whole city is a junk heap. It suits my purpose admirably.'"</span>
  <a data-text-id="5376485330356918010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5841237206640699676">Reading this part of the Penguin Class Deluxe Edition, but thought I'd write a mini-review for the first book. This book was weird, but I like weird things. I also like detective/crime stories, but this isn't really either of those things, even though it is a "detective" fiction. I know that doesn't make sense, but maybe you're not suppose to make sense of this book. This is kind of like a trip down the rabbit hole, but you're Alice. Remember that episode where SpongeBob forget his name tag? Thi</span>
  <span id="freeText5841237206640699676" style="display:none">Reading this part of the Penguin Class Deluxe Edition, but thought I'd write a mini-review for the first book. This book was weird, but I like weird things. I also like detective/crime stories, but this isn't really either of those things, even though it is a "detective" fiction. I know that doesn't make sense, but maybe you're not suppose to make sense of this book. This is kind of like a trip down the rabbit hole, but you're Alice. Remember that episode where SpongeBob forget his name tag? This book is a lot like that episode too. I walked away from the first book wondering what exist and what doesn't exist. Do I exist or is someone writing my story for me? Going to finish the other two books soon. Not sure I want to read Auster's other novels or not. He's might be like some were I like their popular book, but don't really care to continue their others even though I'll add them to-read and possibly read sometime if really want too.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Q7SPK6XkI/+ebhTMBQkSE586oZMuIGHVL6JMsA0olrke6GZWTR5LAu9xkintbAIAMptNqpnpXK48k6f3EUz7dA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QB12spf4FKMxCW/zHOznErLePREs5V+YLPd/gYFGLiIdQZ/PfwJ8XkAW6Rb0ifcBH3/RKJssYuM/xpTGnSJD7w==')})
  }
//]]>

Suddenly, he clapped his hands and the temple gate opened. He went into his work and the gates closed behind him.\' Thus begins Sven Lindqvist\'s profound meditation on art and its relationship with life, first published in 1967, and a classic in his home country - it has never been out of print. As a young man, Sven Lindqvist was fascinated by the myth of Wu Tao-tzu, and by the possibility of entering a work of art and making it a way of life. He was drawn to artists and writers who shared this vision, especially Hermann Hesse, in his novel Glass Bead Game. Partly inspired by Hesse\'s work, Lindqvist lived in China for two years, learning classical calligraphy from a master teacher. There he was drawn deeper into the idea of a life of artistic perfectionism and retreat from the world. But when he left China for India and then Afghanistan, and saw the grotesque effects of poverty and extreme inequality, Lindqvist suffered a crisis of confidence and started to question his ideas about complete immersion in art at the expense of a proper engagement with life. The Myth of Wu Tao-tzu takes us on a fascinating journey through a young man\'s moral awakening and his grappling with profound questions of aesthetics. It contains the bracing moral anger, and poetic, intensely atmospheric travel writing Lindqvist\'s readers have come to love.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16378343427602330183\" href=\"#\" <span id="freeTextContainer4366448007285780942">Paul Auster is the bestselling author of <em>Report from the Interior</em>, <em>Winter Journal</em>, <em>Sunset Park</em>, Invisible, <em>The Book of Illusions</em>, and <em>The New York Trilogy</em>, among many other works. He has been awarded the Prince of Asturias Prize for Literature, the Prix Médicis Étranger, the Independent Spirit Award, and the Premio Napoli. He is a member of the American Academy of Arts and Letters, the American Ac</span>
  <span id="freeText4366448007285780942" style="display:none">Paul Auster is the bestselling author of <em>Report from the Interior</em>, <em>Winter Journal</em>, <em>Sunset Park</em>, Invisible, <em>The Book of Illusions</em>, and <em>The New York Trilogy</em>, among many other works. He has been awarded the Prince of Asturias Prize for Literature, the Prix Médicis Étranger, the Independent Spirit Award, and the Premio Napoli. He is a member of the American Academy of Arts and Letters, the American Academy of Arts and Sciences, and a Commandeur de l'Ordre des Arts et des Lettres. He lives in Brooklyn, New York.</span>
